</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-Mythic-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mythic_21-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mythic_21-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"His romantic saga, coupled with his defiant nationalism, has made Bose a near-mythic figure, not only in his native Bengal, but across India."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2012210&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2012210-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Bose's heroic endeavor still fires the imagination of many of his countrymen. But like a meteor which enters the earth's atmosphere, he burned brightly on the horizon for a brief moment only."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund2004311&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund2004311-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"But in death [Bose] was a martyred patriot whose memory could be an ideal tool for political mobilization."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Bose chose to embrace the fascist powers as allies against the British and fled India, first to Hitler's Germany, then, on a German submarine, to a Japanese-occupied Singapore."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2012210&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2012210-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The deaths of Subhas Chandra Bose in August 1945 and Vallabhbhai Patel in December 1950 removed not only Nehru's principal competitors for national leadership but also powerful competitors for authoritarian state ideologies... Bose thought Hitler and Mussolini represented the wave of the future and would win the war they both anticipated...Styling himself <i>Netaji</i> (leader on the Fuhrer mode), [Bose] declared his objective to be liberation of India by military means.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rudolph-rudolph-bose&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rudolph-rudolph-bose-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Not all Indians, even within the Congress, agreed with the anti-fascist position of the CFD (Congress Foreign Department), and no foreign policy initiative went without contestation. There remained many in India who formally and informally challenged this position by supporting fascist regimes over anti-fascist solidarity. Most prominent was Subhas Chandra Bose, a left-leaning Congressmen from Bengal who famously aligned with and raised an Indian army to support the Axis powers in the Second World War.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Louro-bose&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louro-bose-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-disturbing-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-disturbing_33-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-disturbing_33-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"The most troubling aspect of Bose's presence in Nazi Germany is not military or political but rather ethical. His alliance with the most genocidal regime in history poses serious dilemmas precisely because of his popularity and his having made a lifelong career of fighting the 'good cause'. How did a man who started his political career at the feet of Gandhi end up with Hitler, Mussolini, and Tojo? Even in the case of Mussolini and Tojo, the gravity of the dilemma pales in comparison to that posed by his association with Hitler and the Nazi leadership. The most disturbing issue, all too often ignored, is that in the many articles, minutes, memorandums, telegrams, letters, plans, and broadcasts Bose left behind in Germany, he did not express the slightest concern or sympathy for the millions who died in the concentration camps. Not one of his Berlin wartime associates or colleagues ever quotes him expressing any indignation. Not even when the horrors of Auschwitz and its satellite camps were exposed to the world upon being liberated by Soviet troops in early 1945, revealing publicly for the first time the genocidal nature of the Nazi regime, did Bose react."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHayes2011165&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes2011165-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In early December, pro-Hindu Mahasabha journals published articles in favour of German anti-Semitism. This stance brought the Hindu Mahasabha into conflict with the Congress which, on 12th December, made a statement containing clear references to recent European events. Within the Congress, only Bose opposed the party stance. A few months later, in April 1939, he refused to support the party motion that Jews might find refuge in India.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECasolari202089–90&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECasolari202089–90-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The draft resolution read: 'The Committee sees no objection to the employment in India of such Jewish refugees as are experts and specialists and who can fit in with the new order in India and accept Indian standards.' It was, however, rejected by the then Congress President Subhas Chandra Bose, who four years later in 1942 was reported by the <i>Jewish Chronicle</i> of London as having published an article in <i>Angriff</i>, a journal of Goebbels, saying that "anti-Semitism should become part of the Indian liberation movement because Jews had helped the British to exploit Indians (21 August 1942)" Although by then Bose had left the Congress, he continued to command a strong influence within the party.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-afreedi-2021-bose&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afreedi-2021-bose-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He had opposed Nehru in permitting political asylum to Jews fleeing Europe in 1939. He was prepared to ingratiate himself with Nazi ideology by writing for Goebbels's <i>Der Angriff</i> in 1942. He argued that anti-Semitism should become a factor in the struggle for Indian freedom since the Jews had collaborated with British imperialism to exploit the country and its inhabitants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Shindler-Bose&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shindler-Bose-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bose's anti-Jewish slur was no different from the anti-Semitic remarks in the League deliberations referred to earlier. Bose also opposed Nehru's efforts to provide asylum to a limited number of European Jewish refugees who were fleeing from Nazi persecution."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kumaraswamy-bose&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kumaraswamy-bose-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The INA's battlefield performance was quite poor when assessed either alongside the IJA or against the reformed Fourteenth Army on the battlefields of Assam and Burma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"the campaign revealed that [the INA] was largely a paper tiger."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[The Japanese commander-in-chief Kawabe] still hoped for great things from Bose and the INA, despite all the evidence that both were busted flushes."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011429&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011429-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Another small, but immediate, issue for the civilians in Berlin and the soldiers in training was how to address Subhas Bose. Vyas has given his view of how the term was adopted: 'one of our [soldier] boys came forward with "Hamare Neta". We improved upon it: "Netaji"...&#160;It must be mentioned, that Subhas Bose strongly disapproved of it. He began to yield only when he saw our military group&#160;... firmly went on calling him "Netaji"'. (Alexander) Werth also mentioned adoption of 'Netaji' and observed accurately, that it '...&#160;combined a sense both of affection and honour&#160;...' It was not meant to echo '<a href="/wiki/Fuehrer" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuehrer">Fuehrer</a>' or '<a href="/wiki/Duce" title="Duce">Duce</a>', but to give Subhas Bose a special Indian form of reverence and this term has been universally adopted by Indians everywhere in speaking about him."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Younger Congressmen, including Jawaharlal Nehru, ... thought that constitution-making, whether by the British with their (Simon) Commission or by moderate politicians like the elder (Motilal) Nehru, was not the way to achieve the fundamental changes in society. Nehru and Subhas Bose rallied a group within Congress ... to declare for an independent republic. (p. 305) ... (They) were among those who, impatient with Gandhi's programmes and methods, looked upon socialism as an alternative for nationalistic policies capable of meeting the country's economic and social needs, as well as a link to potential international support (p. 325)."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStein2010305,&#95;325&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein2010305,_325-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Having arrived in Berlin a bruised politician, his broadcasts brought him—and India—world notice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHayes2011162&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes2011162-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"While writing <i>The Indian Struggle</i>, Bose also hired a secretary by the name of Emilie Schenkl. They eventually fell in love and married secretly in accordance with Hindu rites."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHayes201115&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes201115-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Although we must take Emilie Schenkl at her word (about her secret marriage to Bose in 1937), there are a few nagging doubts about an actual marriage ceremony because there is no document that I have seen and no testimony by any other person."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGordon1990344–345&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordon1990344–345-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Tojo turned over all his Indian POWs to Bose's command, and in October 1943 Bose announced the creation of a Provisional Government of Azad ("Free") India, of which he became head of state, prime minister, minister of war, and minister of foreign affairs. Some two million Indians were living in Southeast Asia when the Japanese seized control of that region, and these emigrees were the first "citizens" of that government, founded under the "protection" of Japan and headquartered on the "liberated" Andaman Islands. Bose declared war on the United States and Great Britain the day after his government was established. In January 1944 he moved his provisional capital to Rangoon and started his Indian National Army on their march north to the battle cry of the Meerut mutineers: "Chalo Delhi!"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWolpert2000339&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolpert2000339-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Gracey consoled himself that Bose's Indian National Army had also been in action against his Indians and Gurkhas but had been roughly treated and almost annihilated; when the survivors tried to surrender, they tended to fall foul of the Gurkhas' dreaded kukri."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011295–296&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011295–296-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In May 1945, Bose would fly out of Saigon on an overloaded Japanese plane, headed for Taiwan, which crash-landed and burned. Bose suffered third-degree burns and died in the hospital on <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Formosa</a>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWolpert200969&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolpert200969-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"On 18 August 1945 at Taihoku airport in Taiwan, he died in an air crash, which many Indians still believe never happened."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There are still some in India today who believe that Bose remained alive and in Soviet custody, a once and future king of Indian independence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBaylyHarper200722&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylyHarper200722-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Bose escaped on the last Japanese plane to leave Saigon, but he died in Formosa after a crash landing there in August. By that time, however, his death had been falsely reported so many times that a myth soon emerged in Bengal that Netaji Subhas Chandra was alive—raising another army in China or Tibet or the Soviet Union—and would return with it to "liberate" India.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWolpert2000339–340&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolpert2000339–340-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Subhas Bose was dead, killed in 1945 in a plane crash in the Far East, even though many of his devotees waited—as Barbarossa's disciples had done in another time and in another country—for their hero's second coming."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEChatterji2007278&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChatterji2007278-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As cases began to come to trial, the Indian National Congress began to speak out in defence of INA prisoners, even though it had vocally opposed both the INA's narrative and methods during the war. The Muslim League and the Punjab Unionists followed suit. By mid-September, Nehru was becoming increasingly vocal in his view that trials of INA defendants should not move forward.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarston2014129&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarston2014129-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">What he is remembered for is his vigor, his militancy, his readiness to trade blood (his own if necessary) for nationhood. In large parts of Uttar Pradesh, the historian Gyanendra Pandey has recently remarked, independence is popularly credited not to 'the quiet efforts at self¬regeneration initiated by Mahatma Gandhi,' but to 'the military daring of Netaji Subhas Chandra Bose.'<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFay1995522&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFay1995522-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"On 23 January 1897 at Cuttack, Orissa, was born Subhas Chandra Bose, ninth child of Janakinath and Prabhabati Bose. Janakinath was a lawyer of a Kayastha family, and was wealthy enough to educate all his children well. By Indian standards this family of Bengali origin was well-to-do."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELebra2008a102–103&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELebra2008a102–103-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bose was born into a prominent Bengali family on 23 January 1897 in Cuttack in the present-day state of Orissa. His father was a government pleader who was appointed to the Bengal Legislative Council in 1912."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHayes20111&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes20111-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Despite any whimsy in implementation, the clarity of Gandhi's political vision and the skill with which he carried the reforms in 1920 provided the foundation for what was to follow: twenty-five years of stewardship over the freedom movement. He knew the hazards to be negotiated. The British must be brought to a point where they would abdicate their rule without terrible destruction, thus assuring that freedom was not an empty achievement. To accomplish this he had to devise means of a moral sort, able to inspire the disciplined participation of millions of Indians, and equal to compelling the British to grant freedom, if not willingly, at least with resignation. Gandhi found his means in non-violent <a href="/wiki/Satyagraha" title="Satyagraha">satyagraha</a>. He insisted that it was not a cowardly form of resistance; rather, it required the most determined kind of courage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStein2010297&#95;125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein2010297-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rt. Hon. <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">C. R. Attlee</a>, Prime Minister of Great Britain. Broadcast from London after the assassination of Mahatma Gandhi, 30 January 1948: "For a quarter of a century, this one man has been the major factor in every consideration of the Indian problem."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Attlee-Gandhi-1948-1-30&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Attlee-Gandhi-1948-1-30-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"On 4 November 1937, Subhas sent a letter to Emilie in German, saying that he would probably travel to Europe in the middle of November. "Please write to Kurhaus Hochland, Badgastein," he instructed her, "and enquire if I (and you also) can stay there" He asked her to mention this message only to her parents, not to reply, and wait for his next airmail letter or telegram. On 16 November, he sent a cable: "Starting aeroplane arriving Badgastein twenty second arrange lodging and meet me. ... He spent a month and a half—from 22 November 1937, to 8 January 1938—with Emilie at his favourite resort of Badgastein."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBose2011127&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBose2011127-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"On 26 December 1937, Subhas Chandra Bose secretly married Emilie Schenkl. Despite the obvious anguish, they chose to keep their relationship and marriage a closely guarded secret."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBose2011129–130&#95;145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBose2011129–130-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Rumours that Bose had survived and was waiting to come out of hiding and begin the final struggle for independence were rampant by the end of 1945."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBaylyHarper20072&#95;178-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylyHarper20072-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Fundamental Problems of India" (An address to the Faculty and students of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo University">Tokyo University</a>, November 1944): "You cannot have a so-called democratic system, if that system has to put through economic reforms on a socialistic basis. Therefore we must have a political system—a State—of an authoritarian character. We have had some experience of democratic institutions in India and we have also studied the working of democratic institutions in countries like France, England, and the United States of America. And we have come to the conclusion that with a democratic system we cannot solve the problems of Free India. Therefore, modern progressive thought in India is in favour of a State of an authoritarian character"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBoseBose1997a319–320&#95;202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoseBose1997a319–320-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Bose's heroic endeavor still fires the imagination of many of his countrymen. But like a meteor which enters the earth's atmosphere, he burned brightly on the horizon for a brief moment only."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund2004311&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund2004311-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Subhas Bose might have been a renegade leader who had challenged the authority of the Congress leadership and their principles. But in death he was a martyred patriot whose memory could be an ideal tool for political mobilization."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427&#95;24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBandyopadhyay2004427-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">(p.117) the INA was raised during the Second World War, with the support of the Imperial Japanese Army (IJA); lasted less than three years; and went through two different configurations during that period. In total, it numbered some 40,000 men and women, half of whom are estimated to have been recruited from Indian Army prisoners of war (POWs). The INA's battlefield performance was quite poor when assessed either alongside the IJA or against the reformed Fourteenth Army on the battlefields of Assam and Burma. Reports of its creation in 1942/3 caused consternation among the political and military leadership (p. 118) of the GOI, but in the end its formation did not constitute a legitimate mutiny, and its presence had a negligible impact on the Indian Army.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The (Japanese) Fifteenth Army, commanded by&#160;... Maj.-General Mutuguchi Renya consisted of three experienced infantry divisions—15th, 31st and 33rd—totalling 100,000 combat troops, with the 7,000 strong 1st Indian National Army (INA) Division in support. It was hoped the latter would subvert the Indian Army's loyalty and precipitate a popular rising in British India, but in reality the campaign revealed that it was largely a paper tiger."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The real fault, however, must attach to the Japanese commander-in-chief Kawabe. Dithering, ... prostrated with amoebic dysentery, he periodically reasoned that he must cancel <a href="/wiki/Operation_U-Go" title="Operation U-Go">Operation U-Go</a> in its entirety, but every time he summoned the courage to do so, a cable would arrive from Tokyo stressing the paramount necessity of victory in Burma, to compensate for the disasters in the Pacific. ... Even more incredibly, he still hoped for great things from Bose and the INA, despite all the evidence that both were busted flushes."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011429&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn2011429-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>

cs2"><a href="/wiki/Sekhar_Bandyopadhyay" title="Sekhar Bandyopadhyay">Bandyopadhyay, Sekhar</a> (2004), <i>From Plassey to Partition: A History of Modern India</i>, Hyderabad and Delhi: Orient title="Special:BookSources/81-250-2596-0"><bdi>81-250-2596-0</bdi></a>, <q>The Japanese agreed to provide him transport up to Manchuria from where he could travel to Russia. But on his way, on 18 August 1945 at Taihoku airport in Taiwan, he died in an air crash, which many Indians still believe never happened.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=From+Plassey+to+Partition%3A+A+History+of+Modern+India&amp;rft.place=Hyderabad+and+Delhi&amp;rft.pages=427&amp;rft.pub=Orient+Longmans&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=81-250-2596-0&amp;rft.aulast=Bandyopadhyay&amp;rft.aufirst=Sekhar&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFGilbert2009" class="citation cs2">Gilbert, Martin (2009), <i>The Routledge Atlas of the Second World War</i> (2nd&#160;ed.), Routledge, p.&#160;227, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-55289-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-55289-9"><bdi>978-0-415-55289-9</bdi></a>, <q>Bose died in a plane crash off Taiwan, while being flown to Tokyo on 18 August 1945, aged 48. For many millions of Indians, especially in Bengal, he remains a revered figure</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Routledge+Atlas+of+the+Second+World+War&amp;rft.pages=227&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-55289-9&amp;rft.aulast=Gilbert&amp;rft.aufirst=Martin&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFHuff2020" class="citation cs2">Huff, Gregg (2020), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=waECEAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PR26"><i>World War II and Southeast Asia: Economy and Society under Japanese Occupation</i></a>, Cambridge University Press, p.&#160;xvi, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-09933-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-107-09933-3"><bdi>978-1-107-09933-3</bdi></a>, <a href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://lccn.loc.gov/2020022973">2020022973</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20230712062303/https://books.google.com/books?id=waECEAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PR26">archived</a> from the original on 12 July 2023<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 January</span> 2022</span>, <q>Chronology of World War II in the Pacific: <b>18 August 1945</b> Subhas Chandra Bose killed in a plane crash in Taiwan.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=World+War+II+and+Southeast+Asia%3A+Economy+and+Society+under+Japanese+Occupation&amp;rft.pages=xvi&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Alccn%2F2020022973&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-107-09933-3&amp;rft.aulast=Huff&amp;rft.aufirst=Gregg&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DwaECEAAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPR26&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFSatoshi2012" class="citation cs2">Satoshi, Nakano (2012), <i>Japan's Colonial Moment in Southeast Asia 1942–1945: The Occupiers' Experience</i>, London and New York: Routledge, p.&#160;211, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-138-54128-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-138-54128-3"><bdi>978-1-138-54128-3</bdi></a>, <a href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://lccn.loc.gov/2018026197">2018026197</a>, <q>18 August 1945. Upon hearing of Japan's defeat in the Pacific War, Chandra Bose, who had dedicated his life to the anti-British Indian independence struggle, immediately decided to head for the Soviet Union, "out of my commitment to ally with any country that regards the US and Britain as their enemies." The Japanese Foreign Ministry and the military cooperated in Bose's exile, placing him aboard a Japanese plane headed for Dalian (Yunnan) from Saigon to put him in touch with the Soviet army. After a stopover in Taipei, however, the passenger plane crashed immediately after takeoff. Despite freeing himself from the wreckage, Bose was engulfed in flames and breathed his last.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Japan%27s+Colonial+Moment+in+Southeast+Asia+1942%E2%80%931945%3A+The+Occupiers%27+Experience&amp;rft.place=London+and+New+York&amp;rft.pages=211&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Alccn%2F2018026197&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-138-54128-3&amp;rft.aulast=Satoshi&amp;rft.aufirst=Nakano&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFBlackburnHack2012" class="citation cs2">Blackburn, Kevin; Hack, Karl (2012), <i>War Memory and the Making of Modern Malaysia and Singapore</i>, Singapore: NUS Press, National University of Singapore, p.&#160;185, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-9971-69-599-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-9971-69-599-6"><bdi>978-9971-69-599-6</bdi></a>, <q>Even before the INA memorial was completed, it became the focus of mourning for Singapore's Indian community. The cause of this premature use was news that Bose had died in a plane crash at Taipei, on 18 August. He had been trying to escape capture after the surrender of Japan on 15 August. Singapore and Malaya remained under Japanese control until 5 September when British forces returned. On 26 August 1945, meanwhile, wreaths were laid at the INA memorial in honour of Bose. A large group gathered at the memorial and speeches on Bose's life were made by Major-General M.Z. Kiani and Major-General S.C. Alagappan of the INA, and ITL members. The Japanese newspaper, the Syonan Shimbun, reported that "during the ceremony which lacked nothing in solemnity and dignity, many husky warriors—Sikhs, Punjabis, and others from the Central Provinces—soldiers who had taken part in the actual war operations were seen to shed tears as they saluted for the last time a giant portrait of Netaji which occupied a prominent position in front of the War Memorial".</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=War+Memory+and+the+Making+of+Modern+Malaysia+and+Singapore&amp;rft.place=Singapore&amp;rft.pages=185&amp;rft.pub=NUS+Press%2C+National+University+of+Singapore&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-9971-69-599-6&amp;rft.aulast=Blackburn&amp;rft.aufirst=Kevin&amp;rft.au=Hack%2C+Karl&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFSandler2001" class="citation cs2">Sandler, Stanley, ed. (2001), "Bose, Subhas Chandra (1897–1945)", <i>World War II in the Pacific: An Encyclopedia</i>, Garland Publishing/Routledge, p.&#160;185, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8153-1883-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-8153-1883-9"><bdi>0-8153-1883-9</bdi></a>, <q>Even after the Japanese surrender, Bose was determined to carry on the Free India movement and planned to return to the Subcontinent, despite his renegade status among the British. But on August 18, 1945, the airplane carrying him from Darien to Manchukuo crashed on take off from an airfield in Formosa, and Bose was killed.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Bose%2C+Subhas+Chandra+%281897%E2%80%931945%29&amp;rft.btitle=World+War+II+in+the+Pacific%3A+An+Encyclopedia&amp;rft.pages=185&amp;rft.pub=Garland+Publishing%2FRoutledge&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=0-8153-1883-9&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFBennet1997" class="citation cs2">Bennet, Brad (1997), "Bose, Subhas Chandra (1897–1945)", in Powers, Roger S.; Vogele, William B. (eds.), <i>Protest, Power, and Change: An Encyclopedia of Nonviolent Action from ACT-UP to Women's Suffrage</i>, London and New York: Routledge, p.&#160;48, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8153-0913-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-8153-0913-9"><bdi>0-8153-0913-9</bdi></a>, <q><b>Bose, Subhas Chandra (1897–1945)</b>: Charismatic socialist member of the Indian National Congress and radical anti-imperialist. Bose was born on January 23, 1897, in Cuttack, Bengal, India, and was killed in a plane crash on August 18, 1945.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Bose%2C+Subhas+Chandra+%281897%E2%80%931945%29&amp;rft.btitle=Protest%2C+Power%2C+and+Change%3A+An+Encyclopedia+of+Nonviolent+Action+from+ACT-UP+to+Women%27s+Suffrage&amp;rft.place=London+and+New+York&amp;rft.pages=48&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=0-8153-0913-9&amp;rft.aulast=Bennet&amp;rft.aufirst=Brad&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFMarkovits2021" class="citation cs2">Markovits, Claude (2021), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=aAEbEAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PR19"><i>India and the World: A History of Connections, c. 1750–2000</i></a>, Cambridge University Press, p.&#160;xix, <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="id-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2F9781316899847">10.1017/9781316899847</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-18675-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-107-18675-0"><bdi>978-1-107-18675-0</bdi></a>, <a href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://lccn.loc.gov/2021000608">2021000608</a>, <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:233601747">233601747</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20230712061753/https://books.google.com/books?id=aAEbEAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PR19">archived</a> from the original on 12 July 2023<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 January</span> 2022</span>, <q>Chronology 1945: Indian Army play a major role in the liberation of Burma and Malaya from Japanese occupation; Indian troops sent to receive Japanese capitulation in the Dutch East Indies involved in clashes in Surabaya with Indonesian nationalists opposed to the return of the Dutch; in Indochina, Indian troops help the French re-establish control over Saigon and the south of Vietnam; death of Subhas Bose in a plane crash in Taiwan.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=India+and+the+World%3A+A+History+of+Connections%2C+c.+1750%E2%80%932000&amp;rft.pages=xix&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2021&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2F9781316899847&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A233601747%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Alccn%2F2021000608&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-107-18675-0&amp;rft.aulast=Markovits&amp;rft.aufirst=Claude&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DaAEbEAAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPR19&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite class="citation cs2"><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Alan_Bayly" class="mw-redirect" title="Christopher Alan Bayly">Bayly, Christopher</a>; Harper, Timothy (2007), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0M4Pl_VCExgC"><i>Forgotten Wars: Freedom and Revolution in Southeast Asia</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">Harvard University Press</a>, p.&#160;2, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-674-02153-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-674-02153-2"><bdi>978-0-674-02153-2</bdi></a>, <q>If all else failed (Bose) wanted to become a prisoner of the Soviets: 'They are the only ones who will resist the British. My fate is with them. But as the Japanese plane took off from Taipei airport its engines faltered and then failed. Bose was badly burned in the crash. According to several witnesses, he died on 18 August in a Japanese military hospital, talking to the very last of India's freedom. British and Indian commissions later established convincingly that Bose had died in Taiwan. These were legendary and apocalyptic times, however. Having witnessed the first Indian leader to fight against the British since the great mutiny of 1857, many in both Southeast Asia and India refused to accept the loss of their hero. Rumours that Bose had survived and was waiting to come out of hiding and begin the final struggle for independence were rampant by the end of 1945.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Forgotten+Wars%3A+Freedom+and+Revolution+in+Southeast+Asia&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-674-02153-2&amp;rft.aulast=Bayly&amp;rft.aufirst=Christopher&amp;rft.au=Harper%2C+Timothy&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D0M4Pl&#95;VCExgC&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-gordon-bose-death-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-gordon-bose-death_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-gordon-bose-death_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-gordon-bose-death_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite class="citation cs2"><a href="/wiki/Leonard_A._Gordon" title="Leonard A. Gordon">Gordon, Leonard A.</a> (1990), <i>Brothers Against the Raj: A Biography of Indian Nationalists Sarat and Subhas Chandra Bose</i>, New York and Oxford: Columbia University Press, pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">539–</span>542, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-231-07443-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-231-07443-3"><bdi>0-231-07443-3</bdi></a>, <q>On the plane were: Bose, Shidei, Rahman. Also: Lt. Col. Tadeo Sakai; Lt. Col. Shiro Nonogaki; Major Taro Kono; Major Ihaho Takahashi, Capt. Keikichi Arai, an air force engineer; chief pilot Major Takizawa; co-pilot W/O Ayoagi; navigator Sergeant Okishta; radio-operator NCO Tominaga. The crew was in the front of the aircraft and the passengers were wedged in behind ... there were no proper seats on this aircraft. The plane finally took off (from Saigon) between 5:00 and 5:30 pm on August 17. Since they were so late in starting, the pilot decided to land for the night at Tourane, Vietnam. ... The take-off from Tourane at about 5:00 am was normal ... and they flew to Taipei (Japanese: Taihoku) ...At Taipei ... the crew and passengers took their places ... and they were ready to go at 2:30. ... Just as they left the ground—barely thirty meters up and near the edge of the airfield—there was a loud noise. ... With an enormous crash they hit the ground. ... The injured, including Bose and Rahman and the surviving Japanese officers, were taken to Nanmon Army Hospital. Ground personnel at the airfield had already called the hospital shortly before 3:00 pm and notified Dr. Taneyoshi Yoshimi, the surgeon in charge of the hospital, to prepare to receive the injured. ... Upon arrival the doctor noticed that Bose ... had third degree burns all over his body, but they were worst on his chest. ...Bose and Rahman were quickly taken to the treatment room and the doctor started working on Bose, the much more critically injured man. Dr Yoshimi was assisted by Dr. Tsuruta. ... An orderly, Kazuo Mitsui, an army private, was also in the room, and several nurses were also assisting. ... Bose's condition worsened as the evening darkened. His heart grew weaker. Finally between 9.00 and 10.00 pm, Bose succumbed to his terrible burns.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Brothers+Against+the+Raj%3A+A+Biography+of+Indian+Nationalists+Sarat+and+Subhas+Chandra+Bose&amp;rft.place=New+York+and+Oxford&amp;rft.pages=539-542&amp;rft.pub=Columbia+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1990&amp;rft.isbn=0-231-07443-3&amp;rft.aulast=Gordon&amp;rft.aufirst=Leonard+A.&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTEHayes201115-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayes201115_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayes201115_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayes201115_8-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHayes2011">Hayes 2011</a>, p.&#160;15.</span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-kumaraswamy-bose-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-kumaraswamy-bose_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFKumaraswamy2010" class="citation cs2">Kumaraswamy, P. R. (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=yOHcDwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT153"><i>Squaring the Circle: Mahatma Gandhi and the Jewish National Home, Digital version</i></a>, Routledge, p.&#160;153, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781000097856" title="Special:BookSources/9781000097856"><bdi>9781000097856</bdi></a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220426102940/https://books.google.com/books?id=yOHcDwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT153">archived</a> from the original on 26 April 2022<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 January</span> 2025</span></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Squaring+the+Circle%3A+Mahatma+Gandhi+and+the+Jewish+National+Home%2C+Digital+version&amp;rft.pages=153&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=9781000097856&amp;rft.aulast=Kumaraswamy&amp;rft.aufirst=P.+R.&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DyOHcDwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPT153&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-cronin-2025-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-cronin-2025_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFCronin2025" class="citation book cs1">Cronin, Joseph (2025). "Affiliations, Entanglements, and 'Otherness': The Experiences of German-Speaking Jewish Refugees in India, 1938&#8211;1948". In Cho, Joanne Miyang; Kurlander, Eric; McGechin, Douglas (eds.). <i>German-Speaking Jewish Refugees in Asia, 1930&#8211;1950: Shelter from the Storm?</i>. London and New York: Routledge. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.4324%2F9781003517795-18">10.4324/9781003517795-18</a>. <q>Bose's views on the Nazis' main victims, the Jews, and specifically on the Jewish refugees, were also ambiguous. He wrote to his wife in 1937: "The Jews in Europe have attained so many positions because they are very skilful and the Aryans are very stupid [dumm] - otherwise, how could the foreigners [sic] in Europe make such progress?" Bose also accused his Congress colleague Nehru of "seeking to make India an asylum for Jews" in early 1939, knowing full well that their number would, at most, amount to a few thousand in a population of three hundred million. So, while Bose's opinions did not stem from a place of deep ideological antisemitism, his partial ignorance of the situation for Jews in Germany and Europe at that time, combined with his political allegiances and priorities, led him to suspect that Jewish refugees being sent to India was just another manifestation of Britain flexing its colonial might, of political power play, rather than a reluctant and insufficient response to a rapidly escalating humanitarian crisis.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Affiliations%2C+Entanglements%2C+and+%27Otherness%27%3A+The+Experiences+of+German-Speaking+Jewish+Refugees+in+India%2C+1938%26ndash%3B1948&amp;rft.btitle=German-Speaking+Jewish+Refugees+in+Asia%2C+1930%26ndash%3B1950%3A+Shelter+from+the+Storm%3F&amp;rft.place=London+and+New+York&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2025&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4324%2F9781003517795-18&amp;rft.aulast=Cronin&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118_43-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarston2014117–118_43-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMarston2014">Marston 2014</a>, pp.&#160;117–118.</span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-gordonunrealistic-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-gordonunrealistic_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-gordonunrealistic_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"A number of Japanese officers... saw Bose as a military incompetent as well as an unrealistic and stubborn man who saw only his own needs and problems and could not see the larger picture of the war as the Japanese had to."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGordon1990517&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordon1990517-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-combined-military-lead-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-combined-military-lead_47-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-combined-military-lead_47-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFMarkovits2021" class="citation cs2">Markovits, Claude (2021), <i>India and the World: A History of Connections, c.1750–2000</i>, Cambridge, UK and New York, NY: Cambridge University Press, pp.&#160;79, 113, 114, <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2F9781316899847">10.1017/9781316899847</a>, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-18675-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-107-18675-0"><bdi>978-1-107-18675-0</bdi></a>, <a href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://lccn.loc.gov/2021000609">2021000609</a>, <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:233601747">233601747</a>, <q>(pp. 113–114) y. Amongst the 16,000 Indian prisoners taken by the Axis armies in North Africa, some 3,000 joined the so-called 'Legion of Free India' ('Freies Indien Legion'), in fact the 950th Infantry Regiment of the Wehrmacht, formed in 1942 in response to the call of dissident Indian nationalist leader Subhas Chandra Bose (1897–1945)... As a fighting force, however, the legion proved singularly ineffective...from a strictly military point of view, Bose's attempt was a total fiasco</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=India+and+the+World%3A+A+History+of+Connections%2C+c.1750%E2%80%932000&amp;rft.place=Cambridge%2C+UK+and+New+York%2C+NY&amp;rft.pages=79%2C+113%2C+114&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2021&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2F9781316899847&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A233601747%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Alccn%2F2021000609&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-107-18675-0&amp;rft.aulast=Markovits&amp;rft.aufirst=Claude&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125_48-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoreman2013124–125_48-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMoreman2013">Moreman 2013</a>, pp.&#160;124–125.</span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTEStein2010305,&#95;325-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein2010305,_325_54-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFStein2010">Stein 2010</a>, pp.&#160;305, 325.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-matthews-bose-congress-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-matthews-bose-congress_56-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFMatthews2021" class="citation cs2">Matthews, Roderick (2021), <i>Peace, Poverty, and Betrayal: A New History of British India</i>, Oxford University Press, <q>By this point the Congress leadership was in turmoil after the election of Subhas Chandra Bose as president in 1938. His victory was taken, principally by Bose himself, as proof that Gandhi's star was in decline, and that the Congress could now switch to his personal programme of revolutionary change. He set no store by non-violence and his ideals were pitched a good deal to the left of Gandhi's. His plans also included a large amount of leadership from himself. This autocratic temperament alienated virtually the whole Congress high command, and when he forced himself into the presidency again the next year, the Working Committee revolted. Bose, bitter and broken in health, complained that the 'Rightists' had conspired to bring him down. This was true, but Bose, who seems to have had a talent for misreading situations, seriously overestimated the strength of his support—a significant miscalculation, for it led him to resign in order to create his own faction, the Forward Bloc, modelled on the kind of revolutionary national socialism fashionable across much of Europe at the time.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Peace%2C+Poverty%2C+and+Betrayal%3A+A+New+History+of+British+India&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2021&amp;rft.aulast=Matthews&amp;rft.aufirst=Roderick&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-haithcox-bose-andINC-oldguard-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-haithcox-bose-andINC-oldguard_57-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFHaithcox1971" class="citation cs2">Haithcox, John Patrick (1971), <i>Communism and Nationalism in India: M. N. Roy and Comintern Policy, 1920–1939</i>, Princeton, NJ: Princeton University Press, pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">282–</span>283, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-691-08722-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-691-08722-9"><bdi>0-691-08722-9</bdi></a>, <a href="/wiki/LCCN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="LCCN (identifier)">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://lccn.loc.gov/79120755">79120755</a>, <q>One of the principal points of dispute between Bose and the Congress high command was the attitude the party should take toward the proposed Indian federation. The 1935 Constitution provided for a union of the princely states with the provinces of British India on a federal basis...Following his election for a second term, Bose charged that some members of the Working Committee were willing to compromise on this issue. Incensed at this allegation, all but three of the fifteen members of the Working Committee resigned. The exception was Nehru, Bose himself, and his brother Sarat. There was no longer any hope for reconciliation between the dissidents and the old guard.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Communism+and+Nationalism+in+India%3A+M.+N.+Roy+and+Comintern+Policy%2C+1920%E2%80%931939&amp;rft.place=Princeton%2C+NJ&amp;rft.pages=282-283&amp;rft.pub=Princeton+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1971&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Alccn%2F79120755&amp;rft.isbn=0-691-08722-9&amp;rft.aulast=Haithcox&amp;rft.aufirst=John+Patrick&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASubhas+Chandra+Bose" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTEShanker&#95;Kapoor2017-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShanker_Kapoor2017_197-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFShanker_Kapoor2017">Shanker Kapoor 2017</a>.</span>
